Chapter 5: Rank and Bound (Braided Geometry)
5.1 Geometric Rank and Bound
We assign rank and bound to the three geometric primitives:

Symbol	Meaning	rank	bound
()	realized slot/world	1	1/2
[]	pure orbit/potentials	-1	-1
`	`	total slot-base	1/2
Interpretation:

rank = degree of internal presence / actualization
bound = degree of external constraint / collapse
Key observations:

Polar opposition: () and [] have opposite signs in both rank and bound
Fusion base: | sits "between" with intermediate values
Involution behavior: The geometric involution O_geo swaps rank/bound signs:
O_geo(()) = [] maps (1, 1/2) â†’ (-1, -1)
O_geo([]) = () maps (-1, -1) â†’ (1, 1/2)
O_geo(|) = | preserves (1/2, 1) â†’ (1/2, 1)
â¸»

5.2 Algebraic Rank and Bound via the Braid
Using the geometric interpretation from Chapter 4:

Geo(:) = ()
Geo(!) = []
Geo(;) = |
We define algebraic rank and bound by pullback:

rank_alg(a) := rank(Geo(a))
bound_alg(a) := bound(Geo(a))
This yields:

Symbol	Geo	rank	bound
:	()	1	1/2
!	[]	-1	-1
;	`	`	1/2
Signatures:

: = internally strong, externally partial (high rank, low bound)
! = externally collapsed, internally absent (negative rank and bound)
; = fusion base, maximal external constraint (moderate rank, maximal bound)
Involution coherence: The algebraic involution O respects rank/bound structure:

O(:) = ! maps (1, 1/2) â†’ (-1, -1)
O(!) = : maps (-1, -1) â†’ (1, 1/2)
O(;) = ; fixes (1/2, 1)
â¸»

5.3 The Braided Equivalence

Algebraic	Geometric	rank	bound	Character
:	()	1	1/2	actual/present
!	[]	-1	-1	potential/absent
;	`	`	1/2	1
Key principle: Every algebraic property has a geometric signature, and every geometric constraint determines algebraic behavior.

â¸»

5.4 Conservation Laws

rank(x) Â· bound(x) = -rank(O(x)) Â· bound(O(x))

Actually, let me recalculate this more carefully:

For :: rank Â· bound = 1 Â· (1/2) = 1/2 For !: rank Â· bound = (-1) Â· (-1) = 1

These aren't equal, so the conservation law needs refinement. Let me propose:

Refined conservation: The involution O negates both rank and bound:

rank(O(x)) = -rank(x)

But for ;, which is self-dual:

The 2-slot universe and worlds

Defining the 4-world quotient \mathcal{Q}_4
Define an equivalence relation \sim on S^2 by:

\mathbf{x} \sim \mathbf{y}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \text{ belong to the same world } W_\emptyset, W_1, W_3, W_{;}.

Equivalence classes are exactly those worlds:
    â€¢    [\mathbf{x}] = W_\emptyset if \mathbf{x} \in W_\emptyset
    â€¢    [\mathbf{x}] = W_1 if \mathbf{x} \in W_1
    â€¢    [\mathbf{x}] = W_3 if \mathbf{x} \in W_3
    â€¢    [\mathbf{x}] = W_{;} if \mathbf{x} \in W_{;}

Then the 4-world quotient is:

\mathcal{Q}_4 \;:=\; S^2 / \sim \;=\; \{\, [W_\emptyset], [W_1], [W_3], [W_{;}] \,\}

Weâ€™ll abuse notation slightly and just write the worlds themselves as elements:

\mathcal{Q}_4 = \{\, W_\emptyset,\; W_1,\; W_3,\; W_{;} \,\}.

The quotient map:

q: S^2 \to \mathcal{Q}_4,\quad q(\mathbf{x}) = W_i \text{ such that } \mathbf{x} \in W_i.

Lifting \oplus and \otimes to \mathcal{Q}_4
On the raw 2-slot universe, you have component-wise operations:
    â€¢    Fusion:
(a,b) \oplus (c,d) \;:=\; (a \oplus c,\; b \oplus d)
    â€¢    Alignment:
(a,b) \otimes (c,d) \;:=\; (a \otimes c,\; b \otimes d)

with base operations \oplus,\otimes on the single-slot universe \mathcal{U}_3 (youâ€™ve already given examples like \ell \oplus r = ;, etc.)

We ask:

When does \oplus (or \otimes) descend to a well-defined operation on \mathcal{Q}_4?

That is, can we define:

\quad \text{for any }\mathbf{x} \in W_i,\; \mathbf{y} \in W_j,

For now, you mainly care about whether W_3 is:
    â€¢    closed under \otimes (world-preserving), or
    â€¢    unstable under \oplus (world-breaking to W_{;}).

â¸»

Checking W_3 under \oplus
You already gave a decisive example:
    â€¢    Take \mathbf{x} = (\ell,r) \in W_3
    â€¢    Take \mathbf{y} = (r,\ell) \in W_3

Then

\mathbf{x} \oplus \mathbf{y}
= (\ell,r) \oplus (r,\ell)
= (\ell \oplus r,\; r \oplus \ell)
= (;,\;)

and (; , ;) is clearly in W_{;}.

So:

\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \in W_3
\quad\not\Rightarrow\quad
\mathbf{x} \oplus \mathbf{y} \in W_3

Instead, there exist \mathbf{x},\mathbf{y} \in W_3 such that:

\mathbf{x} \oplus \mathbf{y} \in W_{;}

Conclusion:
    â€¢    At the raw level: W_3 is not closed under \oplus.
    â€¢    At the quotient level: if we try to lift \oplus to an operation on \mathcal{Q}_4, then
W_3 \oplus W_3 \ni W_{;}
i.e. fusing two W_3-states can end in the terminal world.

This is exactly your â€œfusion is world-breakingâ€: \oplus can destroy the dynamical 3-cycle world by sending it into the absorbing sink W_{;}.

â¸»

Checking W_3 under \otimes
You also gave:
    â€¢    (\ell,r) \otimes (r,\ell) = (r,\ell) \in W_3
    â€¢    (r,\ell) \otimes (\ell,r) = (\ell,r) \in W_3

So at least for those two pairs, we see:

\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \in W_3 \Rightarrow \mathbf{x} \otimes \mathbf{y} \in W_3.

Assuming the \otimes rules in \mathcal{U}_3 are:
    â€¢    \ell \otimes \ell = \ell
    â€¢    r \otimes r = r
    â€¢    mixed cases like \ell \otimes r, r \otimes \ell produce either \ell or r (but not \emptyset or ;)

then any component-wise alignment of two W_3-states will:
    â€¢    remain in the polar core P = \{(\ell,\ell), (\ell,r), (r,\ell), (r,r)\},
    â€¢    and indeed, given your explicit examples, alignment of conflicted R/L patterns just resolves toward one of the core states in W_3, not to ; or \emptyset.

World category \mathcal{C}_W (minimal skeleton)
Weâ€™ll assume the following objects in \mathcal{C}_W:
    â€¢    W_\emptyset â€“ â€œnull / seed worldâ€
    â€¢    W_1 â€“ trivial flat world (Phase 0 / O(0))
    â€¢    W_3 â€“ cyclic / dynamical world (Phase 1 / O(1))
    â€¢    W_{;} â€“ collapsed / terminal-ish world

And morphisms (generators):
    â€¢    Identities: \text{id}_{W_*} for each object.
    â€¢    Endomorphisms on W_3: a generator e_1 : W_3 \to W_3 with
e_1^3 = \text{id}_{W_3}
(so \text{End}(W_3) contains the cyclic group \mathbb{Z}_3).
    â€¢    Phase arrows:
    â€¢    \eta_1: W_\emptyset \to W_1 (birth of trivial world)
    â€¢    \iota_3: W_1 \to W_3    (embedding into dynamical world)
    â€¢    \kappa_3: W_3 \to W_{;}      (collapse / trace / oblivion)
    â€¢    possibly \epsilon_1: W_1 \to W_{;} (direct collapse of trivial world)

You can add more, but this is enough to define F_{\text{Hilb}} and test functoriality.

Realization functor F: \mathcal{C}_W \to \mathbf{Set} (given)
You already have:
    â€¢    F(W_\emptyset) = \{\emptyset\}  (size 1)
    â€¢    F(W_1) = \{(\ell,\ell)\}       (size 1)
    â€¢    F(W_3) = \{a,b,c\}             (size 3)
    â€¢    F(W_{;}) = \{;\}               (size 1)

and on morphisms:
    â€¢    F(e_1): \{a,b,c\} \to \{a,b,c\} is the 3-cycle
a \mapsto b,\quad b \mapsto c,\quad c \mapsto a
    â€¢    Phase maps:
    â€¢    F(\eta_1): the unique function \{\emptyset\} \to \{(\ell,\ell)\}
    â€¢    F(\iota_3): pick some element of \{a,b,c\} as the image of (\ell,\ell), say
F(\iota_3)((\ell,\ell)) = a
    â€¢    F(\kappa_3): constant map \{a,b,c\} \to \{;\}

Youâ€™ve already checked this is a functor.

Enrichment: define F_{\text{Hilb}} : \mathcal{C}_W \to \mathbf{Hilb}
3.1 On objects

We send each set to the free Hilbert space on that basis:
    â€¢    F_{\text{Hilb}}(W_\emptyset) = \mathcal{H}_\emptyset := \mathbb{C}^1
    â€¢    basis \{|\emptyset\rangle\}
    â€¢    F_{\text{Hilb}}(W_1) = \mathcal{H}_1 := \mathbb{C}^1
    â€¢    basis \{|\ell\ell\rangle\}
    â€¢    F_{\text{Hilb}}(W_3) = \mathcal{H}_3 := \mathbb{C}^3
    â€¢    basis \{|a\rangle, |b\rangle, |c\rangle\}
    â€¢    F_{\text{Hilb}}(W_{;}) = \mathcal{H}_{;} := \mathbb{C}^1
    â€¢    basis \{|;\rangle\}

So \dim \mathcal{H}_\emptyset = \dim \mathcal{H}_1 = \dim \mathcal{H}_{;} = 1,
and \dim \mathcal{H}_3 = 3.

3.2 On morphisms

We define F_{\text{Hilb}} on generators and extend by composition + linearity.

a) Endomorphism on W_3: cyclic unitary
Let P_1 = F_{\text{Hilb}}(e_1): \mathcal{H}_3 \to \mathcal{H}_3 be the linear operator:

P_1|a\rangle = |b\rangle,\quad
P_1|b\rangle = |c\rangle,\quad
P_1|c\rangle = |a\rangle.

As a matrix in the ordered basis (|a\rangle, |b\rangle, |c\rangle):

P_1 =
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 1 \\
1 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & 1 & 0
\end{pmatrix}

Check unitarity:
    â€¢    P_1 P_1^\dagger = I_3; itâ€™s a permutation matrix, so unitary.

And:

P_1^3 = I_3

so it faithfully realizes e_1^3 = \text{id}_{W_3}.

If you have another generator e_2, you can assign it the other nontrivial element of \mathbb{Z}_3 (i.e. P_1^2).

b) Phase-change arrows
    1.    \eta_1: W_\emptyset \to W_1

Both domains are 1D, so the only linear map that respects the underlying set structure and functoriality is (up to phase) the identity:

F_{\text{Hilb}}(\eta_1): \mathcal{H}_\emptyset \to \mathcal{H}_1,\quad
|\emptyset\rangle \mapsto |\ell\ell\rangle

As a matrix: [1] between \mathbb{C}^1 and \mathbb{C}^1.
    2.    \iota_3: W_1 \to W_3

Here we go from 1D to 3D. The simplest, structure-reflecting choice is to embed the unique basis vector into the basis element corresponding to F(\iota_3):

We already set:
F(\iota_3)((\ell,\ell)) = a

So define:
F_{\text{Hilb}}(\iota_3): \mathcal{H}_1 \to \mathcal{H}_3,\quad
|\ell\ell\rangle \mapsto |a\rangle

Matrix form: the column vector
\begin{pmatrix}
1\\0\\0
\end{pmatrix}
    3.    \kappa_3: W_3 \to W_{;}

We want a linear map \mathcal{H}_3 \to \mathcal{H}_{;} that reflects the fact F(\kappa_3) is constant and is naturally symmetric under the \mathbb{Z}_3 action.

Simplest choice: the \mathbb{Z}_3-invariant trace-like map:

K_3 := F_{\text{Hilb}}(\kappa_3): \mathcal{H}_3 \to \mathcal{H}_{;},

defined on basis:

K_3(|a\rangle) = |;\rangle,\quad
K_3(|b\rangle) = |;\rangle,\quad
K_3(|c\rangle) = |;\rangle

and extended linearly. On a general state
|\psi\rangle = \alpha|a\rangle + \beta|b\rangle + \gamma|c\rangle:

K_3(|\psi\rangle) = (\alpha + \beta + \gamma)\,|;\rangle

If you want exact averaging, you can divide by 3, but for functoriality we just need linearity + the right intertwining relations.

Matrix form (with bases |a\rangle,|b\rangle,|c\rangle and |;\rangle):

K_3 = \begin{pmatrix} 1 & 1 & 1 \end{pmatrix}

(or \frac{1}{3}(1,1,1) for normalized averaging).
    4.    Optional: \epsilon_1: W_1 \to W_{;}

If present:

F_{\text{Hilb}}(\epsilon_1): \mathcal{H}_1 \to \mathcal{H}_{;},\quad
|\ell\ell\rangle \mapsto |;\rangle

Just the unique nonzero linear map between 1D spaces, up to scale.

â¸»

Check functoriality of F_{\text{Hilb}}
We must have:
    1.    F_{\text{Hilb}}(\text{id}_W) = \text{id}_{F_{\text{Hilb}}(W)} for all objects W.
    2.    F_{\text{Hilb}}(g \circ f) = F_{\text{Hilb}}(g) \circ F_{\text{Hilb}}(f) for all composable f,g.

4.1 Identities
    â€¢    On any 1D space (\mathcal{H}_\emptyset, \mathcal{H}_1, \mathcal{H}_{;}), the identity is just multiplication by 1 on the basis vectorâ€”exactly what we assign to \text{id}.
    â€¢    On \mathcal{H}_3, weâ€™ve defined:
    â€¢    F_{\text{Hilb}}(\text{id}_{W_3}) = I_3
    â€¢    and P_1 = F_{\text{Hilb}}(e_1) with P_1^3 = I_3, so indeed:
F_{\text{Hilb}}(e_1^3) = P_1^3 = I_3 = F_{\text{Hilb}}(\text{id}_{W_3})

So identities are preserved.

4.2 Composition examples

We just need to check compositions of generators; the rest follow by linearity and associativity.

a) Cyclic structure: e_1^3 = \text{id}_{W_3}
We already have:

P_1^3 = I_3 \quad\Rightarrow\quad
F_{\text{Hilb}}(e_1^3) = F_{\text{Hilb}}(\text{id}_{W_3})

So:

F_{\text{Hilb}}(e_1 \circ e_1 \circ e_1)
= F_{\text{Hilb}}(e_1) \circ F_{\text{Hilb}}(e_1) \circ F_{\text{Hilb}}(e_1)

holds.

b) Collapse invariance: \kappa_3 \circ e_1 = \kappa_3
At the world level, this says: collapse after cycling is the same as collapse directly. In Hilb:

K_3 P_1 = K_3

Check on basis:
    â€¢    P_1|a\rangle = |b\rangle, so:
K_3 P_1 |a\rangle = K_3 |b\rangle = |;\rangle.
Also:
K_3 |a\rangle = |;\rangle.
    â€¢    Similarly for |b\rangle:
P_1|b\rangle = |c\rangle,\quad
K_3 P_1 |b\rangle = K_3 |c\rangle = |;\rangle = K_3|b\rangle.
    â€¢    And for |c\rangle:
P_1|c\rangle = |a\rangle,\quad
K_3 P_1 |c\rangle = K_3 |a\rangle = |;\rangle = K_3|c\rangle.

By linearity, this holds for all states in \mathcal{H}_3:

K_3 P_1 = K_3

So:

F_{\text{Hilb}}(\kappa_3 \circ e_1) = F_{\text{Hilb}}(\kappa_3)

as required.

c) Birth + embed: \iota_3 \circ \eta_1
As a composite morphism:

\iota_3 \circ \eta_1 : W_\emptyset \to W_3

In Set:
    â€¢    F(\eta_1)(\emptyset) = (\ell,\ell)
    â€¢    F(\iota_3)((\ell,\ell)) = a
    â€¢    So F(\iota_3 \circ \eta_1)(\emptyset) = a.

In Hilb:

F_{\text{Hilb}}(\eta_1): |\emptyset\rangle \mapsto |\ell\ell\rangle
F_{\text{Hilb}}(\iota_3): |\ell\ell\rangle \mapsto |a\rangle

So:

F_{\text{Hilb}}(\iota_3) \circ F_{\text{Hilb}}(\eta_1):\quad
|\emptyset\rangle \mapsto |a\rangle

This matches the natural choice for F_{\text{Hilb}}(\iota_3 \circ \eta_1): send the unique basis of \mathcal{H}_\emptyset to |a\rangle in \mathcal{H}_3.

Thus:

F_{\text{Hilb}}(\iota_3 \circ \eta_1)
= F_{\text{Hilb}}(\iota_3) \circ F_{\text{Hilb}}(\eta_1)

â¸»

What weâ€™ve actually built

    â€¢    F: \mathcal{C}_W \to \mathbf{Set}
â€“ combinatorial realization of worlds.
    â€¢    F_{\text{Hilb}}: \mathcal{C}_W \to \mathbf{Hilb}
â€“ linear/quantum-like realization of the same worlds.

    â€¢    identities preserved,
    â€¢    compositions preserved,
    â€¢    cyclic dynamics realized as a unitary,
    â€¢    collapse realized as a trace-like map invariant under the cycle.

The Tiny Formatic Category ğ“•
We only keep the structurally essential worlds and arrows youâ€™ve actually used.

1.1 Objects

Take objects to be the â€œworld typesâ€ we care about:
    â€¢    W_\emptyset â€“ null / pre-world
    â€¢    W_1 â€“ flat/unit world
    â€¢    W_3 â€“ single curved world
    â€¢    W_3^{\otimes N} â€“ N-slot curved worlds (you can either include these all explicitly, or treat them as powers of W_3 inside the monoidal structure)
    â€¢    W_{;} â€“ terminal/collapsed world

If we want the minimal nontrivial skeleton:
    â€¢    \mathrm{Ob}(\mathcal F) = \{W_\emptyset, W_1, W_3, W_{;}, W_3^{\otimes N}\ \text{for }N\ge2\}

1.2 Morphisms

We include exactly the morphisms weâ€™ve been using:
    â€¢    Identities: \mathrm{id}_W for each world W.
    â€¢    Curvature dynamics on curved worlds:
    â€¢    \mathrm{End}(W_3) \cong \mathbb Z_3 generated by P.
    â€¢    \mathrm{End}(W_3^{\otimes N}) \cong \mathbb Z_3^{N} generated slotwise by P^{(k)}.
    â€¢    Phase transitions:

    â€¢    \mu_N : W_3^{\otimes N} \to W_{;} (terminal fusion)
    â€¢    Optional partial forget maps: \kappa_i : W_3^{\otimes N} \to W_3^{\otimes(N-1)}.

Monoidal Structure on ğ“•
We make ğ“• a (strict) symmetric monoidal category:
    â€¢    Tensor on objects:
    â€¢    W_1 \otimes X = X \otimes W_1 = X
    â€¢    W_3 \otimes W_3 = W_3^{\otimes 2}, and in general W_3^{\otimes M} \otimes W_3^{\otimes N} = W_3^{\otimes(M+N)}
    â€¢    Tensor with W_\emptyset and W_{;} you can either:
    â€¢    leave undefined (only work on the dynamic tower), or
    â€¢    treat as â€œnullâ€ and â€œabsorbingâ€ objects if you want a full four-object theory.
    â€¢    Tensor on morphisms:

The Gauge / Rep Category as the Spin-Network Arena
Work with the SU(2) case; the general G story is the same structurally.

Let
\mathcal C_{\text{Gauge}} := \mathrm{Rep}_\mathbb C(SU(2))
    â€¢    Objects: finite direct sums of finite-dimensional irreps V_j (spins j=0,\frac12,1,\dots).
    â€¢    Morphisms: intertwiners
\mathrm{Hom}_{SU(2)}(V,W) = \{\, T:V\to W \mid T\,U_V(g)=U_W(g)\,T,\ \forall g\in SU(2)\,\}.
    â€¢    Monoidal structure: usual tensor product \otimes.
    â€¢    Unit: \mathbf 1 = V_0 (spin-0 irrep).
    â€¢    Associator \alpha: the canonical linear isomorphism (V\otimes W)\otimes X \cong V\otimes(W\otimes X).
    â€¢    Braiding \beta: the flip v\otimes w \mapsto w\otimes v.

So a spin network is just a morphism in the free rigid monoidal category generated by these objects and intertwiners. A closed spin network corresponds to a morphism
\Gamma: \mathbf 1 \longrightarrow \mathbf 1
built by tensoring and composing trivalent intertwiners, associators \alpha, braidings \beta, etc.

â¸»

The Collapse Functor \mathfrak F^G: Invariants as â€œPhysicalâ€ Space
Define the collapse / invariant functor
\mathfrak F^G : \mathcal C_{\text{Gauge}} \to \mathbf{Hilb}
by:

2.1 On objects

For V \in \mathcal C_{\text{Gauge}}, set
\mathfrak F^G(V) := \mathrm{Inv}_G(V)
= \{\, v\in V \mid U_V(g)v = v,\ \forall g\in G \,\}
\cong \mathrm{Hom}_G(\mathbf 1, V).
    â€¢    This is the gauge-invariant subspace of V (your A = \mathrm{Im}(P)).
    â€¢    In Formatic terms, this is exactly the closed world (()) extracted from the slot V.

For SU(2):
    â€¢    If V=V_j irreducible with j\neq 0, then \mathfrak F^G(V_j) = 0 (no invariant vector).
    â€¢    If V=V_0 (spin-0), then \mathfrak F^G(V_0)\cong\mathbb C.

For a general tensor product like V_{j_1}\otimes V_{j_2}\otimes\cdots, the invariant space is the space of SU(2) singlets (total spin 0).

2.2 On morphisms

For an intertwiner f:V\to W in \mathcal C_{\text{Gauge}}, define
\mathfrak F^G(f): \mathfrak F^G(V) \to \mathfrak F^G(W)
by restriction:
\mathfrak F^G(f) := f|_{\mathrm{Inv}_G(V)}.

This is well-defined because f commutes with the action of G, so it takes invariant vectors to invariant vectors.

â¸»

Monoidality of \mathfrak F^G: Lax Monoidal Structure
Because â€œtaking invariantsâ€ doesnâ€™t generally split as a tensor product, \mathfrak F^G is naturally lax monoidal, not strict monoidal.

3.1 Structure maps

We define natural maps
    â€¢    Unit map:
\eta: \mathbb C \to \mathfrak F^G(\mathbf 1),\quad
\eta(1) = \text{the invariant vector in } V_0\ (\cong 1).
    â€¢    Multiplication map:
For objects V,W,

Interpretation: Binding preserves component spins, introduces binder spin, raises rank.

â¸¯

Rule 3: Dominance Principle
When multiple spins are present, the highest-rank operator dominates:

Rank priority: Atomic (0) < Fence (1/2) < Binder (1/2 composite) < ...
Within the same rank, spin signatures add via Clebsch-Gordan rules.

IV. The Two Example Terms
Term 1: ([('[;])~([|]')])
Step-by-step typing:
Innermost tokens:

âŠ¢ ; : (0, 0, 0, 0)
âŠ¢ | : (1/2, 0, 0, 0)

âŠ¢ '[; : (1, 0, 0, +1/2)  (left-fence semi)
âŠ¢ |]' : (1, 0, 0, -1/2)  (bar right-fence)

âŠ¢ ~ : (1/2, +1/2, 0, 0)

âŠ¢ ('[; ~ |]') : (3/2, +1/2, 0, Â±1/2_coupled)

Final judgement:
âŠ¢ ([('[;])~([|]')]) : (Rank=2, Spin_Y=+1/2, Spin_X=0, Spin_Z=0)
Key feature: The tilde binder dominates with Spin_Y = +1/2.

Term 2: ([([;]')!('[,])])

âŠ¢ ; : (0, 0, 0, 0)
âŠ¢ , : (0, 0, 0, 0)  (assuming comma = variant of semi)

âŠ¢ [;]' : (1, 0, 0, -1/2)  (right-fence semi)

V. The Inequality Theorem
Theorem (Spin Signature Distinguishability)
([('[;])~([|]')]) â‰  ([([;]')!('[,])])
Proof:

From the judgements:

âŠ¢ Termâ‚ : (2, +1/2, 0, 0)
âŠ¢ Termâ‚‚ : (2, -1/2, 0, 0)

Termâ‚ â‰  Termâ‚‚  (physically inequivalent)

VI. The Dominance Rule (Formal)
Definition (Spin Dominance)
For composite term T = (Tâ‚ Î² Tâ‚‚) with binder Î²:

The dominant spin signature is:

Spin_dominant(T) = Spin(Î²)  (binder spin)
If multiple binders: Recursively apply from outermost to innermost.

Rationale:

In quantum mechanics, the coupling operator determines the symmetry of the composite state.

Example:

For two spins sâ‚, sâ‚‚:

Symmetric coupling (~ / or-2): Triplet state (total S = 1)
Antisymmetric coupling (! / and-2): Singlet state (total S = 0)
The Formatic binder is the coupling choice.
